▸ 32.1 Biology and Demography of Ageing 3
▸ 32.1.1 Biology And Physiology Of Ageing

Definition

Ageing is a progressive, universal, and irreversible biological process characterised by progressive loss of physiological function, increased vulnerability to disease, and ultimately death. It involves molecular, cellular, tissue, and systemic changes that accumulate over time.

Core Clinical Content

Ageing definition

Progressive, universal, irreversible decline in physiological function; ↑ vulnerability to disease

Population trends

By 2050, 1 in 6 people globally will be ≥65; fastest growing is ≥80 ('oldest old')

Theories of ageing

Stochastic (random damage) and programmed (genetically determined)

Telomere shortening

Cellular ageing; telomeres shorten with each division; telomerase maintains (cancer risk)

Free radical theory

Mitochondrial ROS damages DNA, proteins, lipids; antioxidants partly protective

DNA damage theory

Accumulated mutations; ↓ DNA repair with age

Somatic mutation theory

Accumulation of mutations drives ageing phenotypes

Inflammageing

Chronic low-grade inflammation with age; ↑ IL-6, TNF-α, CRP

Cellular senescence

Permanent cell cycle arrest; SASP (senescence-associated secretory phenotype)

Caloric restriction

Most consistent intervention to extend lifespan (in animals)

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Biology and physiology of ageing?

Ageing is a progressive, universal, and irreversible biological process characterised by progressive loss of physiological function, increased vulnerability to disease, and ultimately death. It involves molecular, cellular, tissue, and systemic changes that accumulate over time.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Biology and physiology of ageing?

Key features include: ageing definition, population trends, theories of ageing.

Q3. How is Biology and physiology of ageing diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Biology and physiology of ageing?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Biology and physiology of ageing?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Biology and physiology of ageing — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.1.2 Demography And Epidemiology Of Ageing

Definition

The demography of ageing describes the changing population structure with increasing numbers and proportions of older adults globally, driven by declining fertility, increased life expectancy, and population ageing. This has major implications for healthcare, social, and economic systems.

Core Clinical Content

Population ageing

Declining fertility + increased longevity = ↑ median age and proportion of older adults

Demographic transition

Stage 5: low birth rate, low death rate, ageing population (developed countries)

Life expectancy at 65

UK: ~20 more years (men 18, women 21); improving but compressing morbidity

Healthy life expectancy

Years lived in good health; gap with total life expectancy = years with disability

Old age groups

Young-old 65-74, old 75-84, oldest-old ≥85; very old ≥90 ('super-elderly')

Compression of morbidity

Hypothesis: most disability compressed into final years if healthy behaviours

Compression vs expansion

Compression: disability compressed; expansion: more years with disability

Demographic dividend

Economic benefit from large working-age population before ageing

Old-age dependency ratio

≥65 / 15-64; increasing rapidly in developed countries

Implications

↑ chronic disease, dementia, frailty, healthcare costs; need for healthy ageing

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Q1. What is the definition of Demography and epidemiology of ageing?

The demography of ageing describes the changing population structure with increasing numbers and proportions of older adults globally, driven by declining fertility, increased life expectancy, and population ageing. This has major implications for healthcare, social, and economic systems.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Demography and epidemiology of ageing?

Key features include: population ageing, demographic transition, life expectancy at 65.

Q3. How is Demography and epidemiology of ageing diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Demography and epidemiology of ageing?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Demography and epidemiology of ageing?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

▸ 32.1.3 Theories And Mechanisms Of Ageing

Definition

Multiple theories of ageing have been proposed, broadly divided into programmed (genetic, evolutionary) and stochastic (damage) theories. Modern understanding integrates both, with hallmarks including genomic instability, telomere attrition, epigenetic alterations, loss of proteostasis, deregulated nutrient sensing, mitochondrial dysfunction, cellular senescence, stem cell exhaustion, and altered intercellular communication.

Core Clinical Content

12 hallmarks of ageing (López-Otín)

Genomic instability, telomere attrition, epigenetic alterations, loss of proteostasis, deregulated nutrient sensing, mitochondrial dysfunction, cellular senescence, stem cell exhaustion, altered intercellular communication, disabled macroautophagy, chronic inflammation, dysbiosis

Programmed theories

Evolutionary (antagonistic pleiotropy), neuroendocrine, immunological

Antagonistic pleiotropy

Genes beneficial early (reproduction) are harmful late (ageing)

Disposable soma theory

Limited resources for repair vs reproduction; ageing = accumulated damage

Damage theories

Free radical/oxidative stress, DNA damage, error catastrophe, wear and tear

Mitochondrial theory

Mitochondrial dysfunction → ↑ ROS → cellular damage; vicious cycle

Hayflick limit

Cells can only divide ~50 times; related to telomere length

mTOR pathway

Nutrient sensing; inhibited by caloric restriction; mTOR inhibitors (rapamycin) extend lifespan

Sirtuins

NAD+-dependent deacetylases; activated by caloric restriction; SIRT1-7

Senolytics

Drugs that clear senescent cells; potential anti-ageing therapy (in research)

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
12 hallmarks of ageing (López-Otín)Genomic instability, telomere attrition, epigenetic alterations, loss of proteos
Programmed theoriesEvolutionary (antagonistic pleiotropy), neuroendocrine, immunological
Antagonistic pleiotropyGenes beneficial early (reproduction) are harmful late (ageing)
Disposable soma theoryLimited resources for repair vs reproduction; ageing = accumulated damage
Damage theoriesFree radical/oxidative stress, DNA damage, error catastrophe, wear and tear
Mitochondrial theoryMitochondrial dysfunction → ↑ ROS → cellular damage; vicious cycle
Hayflick limitCells can only divide ~50 times; related to telomere length
mTOR pathwayNutrient sensing; inhibited by caloric restriction; mTOR inhibitors (rapamycin)
SirtuinsNAD+-dependent deacetylases; activated by caloric restriction; SIRT1-7
SenolyticsDrugs that clear senescent cells; potential anti-ageing therapy (in research)

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Theories and mechanisms of ageing?

Multiple theories of ageing have been proposed, broadly divided into programmed (genetic, evolutionary) and stochastic (damage) theories. Modern understanding integrates both, with hallmarks including genomic instability, telomere attrition, epigenetic alterations, loss of proteostasis, deregulated nutrient sensing, mitochondrial dysfunction, cellular senescence, stem cell exhaustion, and altered intercellular communication.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Theories and mechanisms of ageing?

Key features include: 12 hallmarks of ageing (lópez-otín), programmed theories, antagonistic pleiotropy.

Q3. How is Theories and mechanisms of ageing diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Theories and mechanisms of ageing?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Theories and mechanisms of ageing?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Theories and mechanisms of ageing — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.10 Pressure Ulcers and Skin 2
▸ 32.10.1 Pressure Ulcers Pressure Sores In The Elderly

Definition

Pressure ulcers (also called pressure sores, decubitus ulcers, or bedsores) are localised areas of tissue damage caused by sustained pressure, shear, friction, or moisture, typically over bony prominences. They are common in immobile, frail elderly and cause significant morbidity, mortality, and healthcare cost.

Core Clinical Content

Pressure ulcer prevalence

10-15% of hospitalised elderly; 20-30% of nursing home; ↑ in critical care, palliative

Mechanism

Sustained pressure > capillary pressure (32 mmHg) → ischaemia → tissue necrosis

Risk factors

Immobility, ↓ sensation, ↓ perfusion, malnutrition, dehydration, incontinence, age-related skin changes

Sites

Sacrum, heels, trochanters, ischial tuberosities, elbows, occiput

NPUAP/EPUAP staging (1-4)

1: non-blanchable erythema; 2: partial thickness; 3: full thickness; 4: full thickness + exposed bone/tendon

Unstageable

Full thickness obscured by slough or eschar; needs debridement to stage

Suspected deep tissue injury (SDTI)

Purple/maroon localised area or blood-filled blister from pressure/shear

Prevention (SSKIN)

Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence/moisture, Nutrition/hydration

Pressure redistribution

Specialised mattresses, cushions; 2-hourly repositioning

Management

Stage-appropriate dressing (hydrocolloid, foam, alginate); debridement; infection control; nutrition

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Pressure ulcers (pressure sores) in the elderly?

Pressure ulcers (also called pressure sores, decubitus ulcers, or bedsores) are localised areas of tissue damage caused by sustained pressure, shear, friction, or moisture, typically over bony prominences. They are common in immobile, frail elderly and cause significant morbidity, mortality, and healthcare cost.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Pressure ulcers (pressure sores) in the elderly?

Key features include: pressure ulcer prevalence, mechanism, risk factors.

Q3. How is Pressure ulcers (pressure sores) in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Pressure ulcers (pressure sores) in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Pressure ulcers (pressure sores) in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Pressure ulcers (pressure sores) in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.10.2 Skin Problems And Wound Care In The Elderly

Definition

Skin problems are common in the elderly due to age-related skin changes (xerosis, atrophy, ↓ barrier function) and comorbidities. Wound healing is impaired in older adults, requiring careful assessment and management of common conditions including pruritus, eczema, leg ulcers, and skin tears.

Core Clinical Content

Age-related skin changes

↓ Collagen, ↓ elastin, ↓ subcutaneous fat, ↓ barrier function, ↓ sweat, ↓ sebum

Xerosis (dry skin)

Very common in elderly; pruritus; treatment: emollients, avoid hot water, ↑ humidity

Pruritus (itch)

Common; causes: xerosis, eczema, scabies, drug, liver, renal, haematological

Skin tears

Common in elderly; fragile skin; from minor trauma; prevention: protective clothing, careful handling

Venous leg ulcer

Common; medial gaiter area; associated with venous disease; compression bandaging

Arterial leg ulcer

Distal (toes, lateral ankle); painful, pale base; poor healing; revascularisation

Diabetic foot ulcer

Neuroischaemic; high risk of infection/amputation; multidisciplinary foot clinic

Pressure ulcers (covered separately)

Sacrum, heels; staged; prevention by repositioning

Basal cell carcinoma

Most common skin cancer in elderly; pearly papule, telangiectasias; sun-exposed areas

Wound healing

↓ in elderly; chronic wounds (>3 months); pressure, infection, diabetes, malnutrition delay healing

▸ 32.11 Sensory and Functional Decline 3
▸ 32.11.1 Functional Decline And Rehabilitation In The Elder

Definition

Functional decline in the elderly is the loss of ability to perform activities essential to independent living, often triggered by acute illness, hospitalisation, or decompensation of chronic disease. Comprehensive rehabilitation addresses physical, cognitive, and social domains to optimise independence and quality of life.

Core Clinical Content

Functional decline

Loss of ADL/IADL; often triggered by acute illness; ↑ in hospitalised elderly

Hospital-associated deconditioning

Bed rest → ↓ muscle mass 1-5%/day; ↓ aerobic capacity; common in elderly

ADL vs IADL

ADL: basic (bathing, dressing, toileting, transferring, continence, feeding); IADL: complex (cooking, shopping)

Comprehensive rehabilitation

Multidisciplinary: PT (mobility, strength), OT (ADL, IADL), SALT (swallowing, communication)

Goals of rehabilitation

Maximise independence, quality of life, function; realistic goal-setting with patient/family

Strength training in elderly

Safe, effective; improves function, falls, mortality; 2-3 times/week

Balance training

Tai chi, Otago; ↓ falls; improves confidence, mobility

Home assessment

OT home visit; remove hazards; equipment; adapt environment

Discharge planning

Early, multidisciplinary; consider home vs intermediate care vs residential care

Predictors of poor recovery

Advanced age, dementia, frailty, multiple comorbidities, malnutrition, severe disability

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Functional decline and rehabilitation in the elderly?

Functional decline in the elderly is the loss of ability to perform activities essential to independent living, often triggered by acute illness, hospitalisation, or decompensation of chronic disease. Comprehensive rehabilitation addresses physical, cognitive, and social domains to optimise independence and quality of life.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Functional decline and rehabilitation in the elderly?

Key features include: functional decline, hospital-associated deconditioning, adl vs iadl.

Q3. How is Functional decline and rehabilitation in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Functional decline and rehabilitation in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Functional decline and rehabilitation in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Functional decline and rehabilitation in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.11.2 Hearing Impairment In The Elderly

Definition

Hearing impairment (presbycusis and conductive losses) is a common age-related condition with significant impact on communication, cognition, and quality of life in older adults. It is associated with social isolation, depression, and cognitive decline.

Core Clinical Content

Hearing loss prevalence

33% of ≥65; 50% of ≥75; 80% of nursing home residents

Presbycusis

Age-related bilateral sensorineural high-frequency loss; gradual; due to hair cell loss in cochlea

Conductive hearing loss

Outer/middle ear pathology; wax (very common, easily treated), otitis media, otosclerosis

Sudden sensorineural hearing loss

ENT emergency; treat with high-dose steroids within 48 h; consider acoustic neuroma

Cerumen impaction

Very common in elderly; causes 30% of hearing aid failure; treat with ear drops or irrigation

Ototoxic drugs

Aminoglycosides, loop diuretics, cisplatin, quinine; monitor in elderly

Hearing aids

Underused; improve quality of life, cognition, social participation; consider for ≥30 dB loss

Tinnitus

Common in elderly; often with presbycusis; CBT, sound therapy; rule out acoustic neuroma

Impact of hearing loss

↑ Social isolation, ↑ depression, ↑ cognitive decline (3-5x dementia risk), ↑ falls

Communication strategies

Face patient, reduce background noise, speak slowly, consider hearing loop

Flashcards

Q: Hearing loss prevalence?

A: 33% of ≥65; 50% of ≥75; 80% of nursing home residents

Q: Presbycusis?

A: Age-related bilateral sensorineural high-frequency loss; gradual; due to hair cell loss in cochlea

Q: Conductive hearing loss?

A: Outer/middle ear pathology; wax (very common, easily treated), otitis media, otosclerosis

Q: Sudden sensorineural hearing loss?

A: ENT emergency; treat with high-dose steroids within 48 h; consider acoustic neuroma

Q: Cerumen impaction?

A: Very common in elderly; causes 30% of hearing aid failure; treat with ear drops or irrigation

Q: Ototoxic drugs?

A: Aminoglycosides, loop diuretics, cisplatin, quinine; monitor in elderly

Q: Hearing aids?

A: Underused; improve quality of life, cognition, social participation; consider for ≥30 dB loss

Q: Tinnitus?

A: Common in elderly; often with presbycusis; CBT, sound therapy; rule out acoustic neuroma

▸ 32.11.3 Visual Impairment In The Elderly

Definition

Visual impairment is a significant cause of disability and reduced quality of life in the elderly, with age-related changes affecting all ocular structures. Common conditions include cataract, age-related macular degeneration (AMD), glaucoma, and diabetic retinopathy.

Core Clinical Content

Visual impairment prevalence

Significant visual impairment in 5-10% of ≥65; 20% of ≥85; ↑ with age

Cataract

Lens opacity; most common cause reversible visual loss; risk ↑ age, smoking, DM, steroids

Cataract symptoms

Gradual painless ↓ vision, glare, ↓ colour perception; surgical removal + lens implant

Age-related macular degeneration (AMD)

Central vision loss; drusen; risk: age, smoking, family history; ARED-2 vitamins may slow progression

AMD types

Dry (90% — drusen, atrophy; no treatment); Wet (10% — neovascularisation; anti-VEGF injection)

Glaucoma

↑ Intraocular pressure → optic nerve damage; chronic open-angle (most common in elderly)

Glaucoma symptoms

Peripheral vision loss first; tunnel vision; treatment: drops (prostaglandin), laser, surgery

Diabetic retinopathy

Microaneurysms, exudates, haemorrhages, neovascularisation; screening annually; anti-VEGF for wet

Other (elderly)

Dry eye (very common), blepharitis, ptosis, presbyopia

Impact of visual loss

↑ Falls, ↑ depression, ↑ social isolation, ↑ cognitive decline; importance of early detection

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Visual impairment in the elderly?

Visual impairment is a significant cause of disability and reduced quality of life in the elderly, with age-related changes affecting all ocular structures. Common conditions include cataract, age-related macular degeneration (AMD), glaucoma, and diabetic retinopathy.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Visual impairment in the elderly?

Key features include: visual impairment prevalence, cataract, cataract symptoms.

Q3. How is Visual impairment in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Visual impairment in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Visual impairment in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Visual impairment in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.12 Sleep Disorders 1
▸ 32.12.1 Sleep Disorders In The Elderly

Definition

Sleep disorders are common in older adults, with changes in sleep architecture, increased prevalence of insomnia, sleep apnoea, restless legs syndrome, and other conditions. Sleep disturbance has major impact on quality of life, cognition, falls, and mood in the elderly.

Core Clinical Content

Age-related sleep changes

↓ Slow wave sleep, ↓ sleep efficiency, ↑ sleep fragmentation, phase advance (earlier bedtime/wake)

Insomnia prevalence in elderly

20-30% of community elderly; ↑ in nursing home, dementia, depression

Insomnia causes

Medical (pain, nocturia, dyspnoea), psychiatric (depression, anxiety), medications, primary sleep disorders

Sleep apnoea in elderly

↑ Prevalence; OSA associated with ↑ cardiovascular risk, daytime sleepiness, cognitive decline

Restless legs syndrome (RLS)

Common in elderly; urge to move legs, worse at rest/evening; iron deficiency common

REM sleep behaviour disorder (RBD)

Acting out dreams; common in neurodegenerative diseases (Parkinson's, DLB)

Sleep and dementia

Sleep-wake cycle disruption common; sundowning; improve sleep hygiene

Sleep and depression

Bidirectional; insomnia ↑ depression risk; depression disturbs sleep architecture

Non-pharmacological

Sleep hygiene, CBT-I, ↑ daytime light, ↓ caffeine, regular schedule

Pharmacological

Melatonin first-line; short-term Z-drugs (zopiclone) if needed; AVOID long-term benzodiazepines

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Sleep disorders in the elderly?

Sleep disorders are common in older adults, with changes in sleep architecture, increased prevalence of insomnia, sleep apnoea, restless legs syndrome, and other conditions. Sleep disturbance has major impact on quality of life, cognition, falls, and mood in the elderly.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Sleep disorders in the elderly?

Key features include: age-related sleep changes, insomnia prevalence in elderly, insomnia causes.

Q3. How is Sleep disorders in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Sleep disorders in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Sleep disorders in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Sleep disorders in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SIG E CAPS' for depression in elderly (atypical presentation common)
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty
  • 'Hot as a hare, dry as a bone, red as a beet, blind as a bat, mad as a hatter' — anticholinergic syndrome
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.13 Specific Geriatric Conditions 3
▸ 32.13.1 Hip Fracture And Orthogeriatric Care

Definition

Hip fracture is a fracture of the proximal femur, common in older adults usually due to falls from standing height (fragility fracture). Orthogeriatric care is a collaborative model between orthopaedic surgeons and geriatricians, with evidence of improved outcomes for older patients with hip fracture.

Core Clinical Content

Hip fracture prevalence

~70,000/year UK; most common fragility fracture; 90% in ≥65; F:M 3:1

Hip fracture types

Intracapsular (subcapital, transcervical) and extracapsular (intertrochanteric, subtrochanteric)

Clinical presentation

Fall, hip pain, shortened and externally rotated leg, unable to weight-bear

Investigations

X-ray (AP pelvis); CT/MRI if X-ray negative but high suspicion

Management — early surgery

Within 36 hours of admission; ↓ mortality, ↓ complications, ↓ length of stay

Surgical options — intracapsular

Displaced (>50%): hemiarthroplasty or total hip replacement (THR); undisplaced: internal fixation

Surgical options — extracapsular

Internal fixation (DHS, IM nail); depends on fracture pattern and bone quality

Orthogeriatric care

Joint orthopaedic-geriatric management; evidence-based; improves outcomes

Pre-operative optimisation

Correct anaemia, anticoagulation reversal, hydration, comorbidities

Complications

Delirium (most common), VTE, infection, pressure sores, mortality 10% at 30 days, 30% at 1 year

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Hip fracture and orthogeriatric care?

Hip fracture is a fracture of the proximal femur, common in older adults usually due to falls from standing height (fragility fracture). Orthogeriatric care is a collaborative model between orthopaedic surgeons and geriatricians, with evidence of improved outcomes for older patients with hip fracture.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Hip fracture and orthogeriatric care?

Key features include: hip fracture prevalence, hip fracture types, clinical presentation.

Q3. How is Hip fracture and orthogeriatric care diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Hip fracture and orthogeriatric care?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Hip fracture and orthogeriatric care?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Hip fracture and orthogeriatric care — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.13.2 Hypothermia Hyperthermia And Age Related Thermal D

Definition

Elderly patients have impaired thermoregulation due to age-related physiological changes, comorbidities, and medications, making them vulnerable to both hypothermia and hyperthermia, which are associated with significant morbidity and mortality.

Core Clinical Content

Thermoregulation in elderly

↓ Basal metabolic rate, ↓ shivering response, ↓ sweating, ↓ perception of temperature

Hypothermia

Core temp <35°C; mortality 30-50% in elderly; often missed

Hypothermia causes

Cold exposure, immobility, sepsis, hypothyroidism, drugs (sedatives, alcohol)

Hypothermia signs

Confusion, bradycardia, hypotension, slow respiration, cold, pale skin; ↓ shivering in elderly

Hypothermia management

Warm environment, warm IV fluids, warming blanket; gentle re-warming; treat cause

Hyperthermia

Core temp >38°C; sepsis, heat stroke, drug reaction, thyroid storm

Heat stroke

Core >40°C; CNS dysfunction; hot dry skin; emergency; rapid cooling; ↑ mortality in elderly

Drug-related thermoregulation

Antipsychotics (NMS), anticholinergics (↓ sweating), SSRIs (serotonin syndrome), anaesthetics

Fever in elderly

Often blunted or absent; altered mental status may be only sign; consider sepsis

Prevention

Home temperature monitoring; appropriate clothing; hydration; medication review

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.13.3 Osteoporosis And Falls Related Fractures In The El

Definition

Osteoporosis is a systemic skeletal disease characterised by low bone mass and microarchitectural deterioration of bone tissue, leading to increased bone fragility and fracture risk. Fragility fractures (especially hip, vertebra, wrist) are a major cause of morbidity and mortality in older adults.

Core Clinical Content

Osteoporosis definition

T-score ≤-2.5 SD on DEXA; osteopenia -1 to -2.5

Prevalence

50% of women, 20% of men ≥50; ↑ with age

Risk factors (non-modifiable)

Age, female, early menopause, family history, Caucasian/Asian, low BMI

Risk factors (modifiable)

Smoking, alcohol, sedentary, low Ca/vit D, low weight, glucocorticoids

DEXA scan

Gold standard; measures BMD at hip and spine; T-score comparison to young adult

FRAX score

10-year fracture risk calculator; combines clinical risk factors ± BMD; guides treatment

Fragility fractures

Hip, vertebra, wrist, proximal humerus, pelvis; from low-energy trauma (fall from standing)

Hip fracture

90-day mortality 20-30%; surgery within 36 h; early mobilisation; MDT care

Vertebral fracture

Often asymptomatic; back pain, height loss, kyphosis; may cause restrictive lung disease

Treatment

Bisphosphonates (alendronate, zoledronic acid), denosumab, teriparatide; Ca + vit D supplementation

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
Osteoporosis definitionT-score ≤-2.5 SD on DEXA; osteopenia -1 to -2.5
Prevalence50% of women, 20% of men ≥50; ↑ with age
Risk factors (non-modifiable)Age, female, early menopause, family history, Caucasian/Asian, low BMI
Risk factors (modifiable)Smoking, alcohol, sedentary, low Ca/vit D, low weight, glucocorticoids

Vertebral fractureOften asymptomatic; back pain, height loss, kyphosis; may cause restrictive lung
TreatmentBisphosphonates (alendronate, zoledronic acid), denosumab, teriparatide; Ca + vi

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Osteoporosis and falls-related fractures in the elderly?

Osteoporosis is a systemic skeletal disease characterised by low bone mass and microarchitectural deterioration of bone tissue, leading to increased bone fragility and fracture risk. Fragility fractures (especially hip, vertebra, wrist) are a major cause of morbidity and mortality in older adults.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Osteoporosis and falls-related fractures in the elderly?

Key features include: osteoporosis definition, prevalence, risk factors (non-modifiable).

Q3. How is Osteoporosis and falls-related fractures in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Osteoporosis and falls-related fractures in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Osteoporosis and falls-related fractures in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Osteoporosis and falls-related fractures in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.2 Cognitive Issues in the Elderly 3
▸ 32.2.1 Behavioural And Psychological Symptoms Of Dementia

Definition

Behavioural and psychological symptoms of dementia (BPSD) are non-cognitive symptoms of dementia, including agitation, aggression, psychosis, mood disturbance, sleep disturbance, and wandering. They are common, cause significant distress, and are often treatable.

Core Clinical Content

BPSD definition

Non-cognitive symptoms of dementia; common (~90% over disease course)

BPSD categories

Behavioural (agitation, aggression, wandering), psychological (psychosis, mood, anxiety)

BPSD causes

Pain, infection, constipation, dehydration, medication, environment, depression

Non-pharmacological first

Person-centred care, environment, activities, communication; first-line

ABC approach

Antecedent, Behaviour, Consequence; identify triggers and patterns

Antipsychotic use

Limited to severe agitation/aggression; lowest dose, shortest time; black box warning

Antipsychotic risks in dementia

↑ Mortality, ↑ cerebrovascular events, ↑ pneumonia, ↑ falls

Antidepressants

SSRIs for depression, anxiety, agitation in dementia; citalopram may help agitation

Benzodiazepines

AVOID; ↑ falls, confusion, paradoxical agitation; reserve for alcohol withdrawal

Cholinesterase inhibitors

May help BPSD (especially apathy, depression) in Alzheimer's; modest effect

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Behavioural and psychological symptoms of dementia (BPSD) — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.2.2 Depression In The Elderly

Definition

Depression in the elderly is a mood disorder characterised by persistent low mood, anhedonia, and other depressive symptoms occurring in older adults, often with atypical presentation, comorbidities, and significant impact on function and mortality. It is the most common mental health problem in the elderly but is often under-recognised and under-treated.

Core Clinical Content

Depression prevalence in elderly

10-15% of community elderly; 30-40% in hospitalised; commonest mental health problem

Risk factors

Female, social isolation, bereavement, chronic illness, disability, recent hospitalisation

Atypical presentation

Somatic symptoms, anxiety, pseudodementia, ↓ motivation, irritability; less overt sadness

Pseudodementia

Cognitive impairment from depression; reversible with treatment; 'I don't know' answers

Differential from dementia

Depression: abrupt onset, 'don't know', mood congruent, reversible; dementia: gradual, confabulation

Differential from grief

Grief: waves, focused on loss; depression: pervasive, anhedonia, suicidal

Suicide risk

Highest in elderly men; completed suicide rate highest in ≥75; means more lethal

Investigation

Exclude organic causes (TFT, Ca, B12, glucose, FBC); PHQ-9, Geriatric Depression Scale

First-line treatment

SSRI (sertraline, citalopram); avoid TCAs (anticholinergic, falls, cardiac)

SSRI cautions in elderly

Hyponatraemia (SIADH), bleeding, falls, QTc (citalopram), discontinuation syndrome

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Depression in the elderly?

Depression in the elderly is a mood disorder characterised by persistent low mood, anhedonia, and other depressive symptoms occurring in older adults, often with atypical presentation, comorbidities, and significant impact on function and mortality. It is the most common mental health problem in the elderly but is often under-recognised and under-treated.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Depression in the elderly?

Key features include: depression prevalence in elderly, risk factors, atypical presentation.

Q3. How is Depression in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Depression in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Depression in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Depression in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SIG E CAPS' for depression in elderly (atypical presentation common)
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty
  • 'Hot as a hare, dry as a bone, red as a beet, blind as a bat, mad as a hatter' — anticholinergic syndrome
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.2.3 Mild Cognitive Impairment Mci And Dementia In The

Definition

Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) is a state of objectively measurable cognitive decline that is not severe enough to interfere significantly with daily activities. Dementia is an acquired, progressive cognitive decline severe enough to affect daily functioning. Both are common in older adults and require comprehensive assessment.

Core Clinical Content

MCI definition

Subjective + objective cognitive decline; preserved ADL; not dementia

MCI prevalence

15-20% of ≥65; 5-15% progress to dementia per year

MCI types

Amnestic (↑ risk Alzheimer's) vs non-amnestic (↑ risk other dementias)

Reversible causes of cognitive impairment

DELIRIUM, Depression, Drugs, Deficiencies (B12, folate, thyroid), Normal pressure hydrocephalus

Differential diagnosis

Dementia, delirium, depression, drugs, metabolic, neurological

Investigation

Bloods (TFT, B12, folate, calcium), CT/MRI, MMSE/MoCA, neuropsychology

Dementia types

Alzheimer's (60-70%), vascular (15-25%), Lewy body (10-15%), frontotemporal (5-10%)

Mild cognitive impairment management

Risk factor modification (BP, lipids, exercise), cognitive training, monitor

Prognosis

MCI: 5-15% progress/year; risk higher with APOE4, vascular risk factors

Driving and dementia

DVLA must be notified; assess by specialist; usually cease driving

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) and dementia in the elderly?

Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) is a state of objectively measurable cognitive decline that is not severe enough to interfere significantly with daily activities. Dementia is an acquired, progressive cognitive decline severe enough to affect daily functioning. Both are common in older adults and require comprehensive assessment.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) and dementia in the elderly?

Key features include: mci definition, mci prevalence, mci types.

Q3. How is Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) and dementia in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) and dementia in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) and dementia in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Mild cognitive impairment (MCI) and dementia in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.3 Continence and Elimination 2
▸ 32.3.1 Faecal Incontinence And Constipation In The Elderl

Definition

Faecal incontinence is the involuntary loss of stool, often due to overflow from constipation in older adults. Constipation is reduced bowel frequency (<3 per week) or difficulty passing stool, very common in the elderly and a frequent cause of morbidity including overflow, urinary retention, falls, and confusion.

Core Clinical Content

Constipation prevalence

30-40% of community elderly; 50-70% of nursing home; ↑ with age, women

Constipation causes

Drugs (opioids, anticholinergics, calcium), immobility, low fibre, dehydration, hypothyroidism

Faecal impaction

Hard stool in rectum; cause of overflow incontinence, urinary retention, confusion

Faecal incontinence causes

Overflow (impaction), sphincter weakness, neurological (stroke, dementia), severe diarrhoea

Red flags (constipation)

Rectal bleeding, weight loss, iron-deficiency anaemia, change in bowel habit → exclude colorectal cancer

Assessment

History, abdominal and rectal examination, bloods (TFT, Ca), colonoscopy if red flags

Laxative types

Bulking (ispaghula), osmotic (lactulose, macrogol), stimulant (senna), rectal (suppository, enema)

Constipation management

Fibre, fluids, exercise; stimulant (senna) + osmotic (macrogol) first-line

Faecal incontinence management

Treat cause (impaction); barrier creams; bowel training; biofeedback

Opioid-induced constipation

Common; prophylactic laxatives; consider methylnaltrexone (peripheral opioid antagonist)

▸ 32.3.2 Urinary Incontinence In The Elderly

Definition

Urinary incontinence is the involuntary loss of urine, a common and distressing condition in older adults, often multifactorial, with significant impact on quality of life, function, and caregiver burden. It is rarely a single disease but a symptom with multiple contributing factors.

Core Clinical Content

UI prevalence

10-20% of community elderly; 50% of nursing home residents; ↑ with age, women > men

UI types

Stress, urge, overflow, functional, mixed (most common in elderly)

Stress incontinence

Leakage with cough, sneeze, effort; weak pelvic floor; common in women post-childbirth

Urge incontinence

Sudden urge, frequency, nocturia; overactive bladder; detrusor overactivity

Overflow incontinence

Dribbling, incomplete emptying, palpable bladder; retention; prostate disease, neuropathy

Functional incontinence

Cognitive/mobility impairment prevents reaching toilet; often in dementia, immobility

Transient causes (DIAPPERS)

Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction

Assessment

History (3-day bladder diary), examination, urinalysis, post-void residual, urodynamic studies

Management

Pelvic floor exercises, bladder training, weight loss; oxybutynin/mirabegron (urge); catheter (overflow)

UTI association

Asymptomatic bacteriuria common in elderly; do NOT treat unless symptomatic

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Urinary incontinence in the elderly?

Urinary incontinence is the involuntary loss of urine, a common and distressing condition in older adults, often multifactorial, with significant impact on quality of life, function, and caregiver burden. It is rarely a single disease but a symptom with multiple contributing factors.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Urinary incontinence in the elderly?

Key features include: ui prevalence, ui types, stress incontinence.

Q3. How is Urinary incontinence in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Urinary incontinence in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Urinary incontinence in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Urinary incontinence in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.4 Falls and Syncope 4
▸ 32.4.1 Falls In The Elderly

Definition

A fall is an event resulting in a person coming to rest inadvertently on the ground or floor, often with injury. Falls are a major cause of morbidity, mortality, and loss of independence in older adults, with multifactorial aetiology requiring comprehensive assessment.

Core Clinical Content

Falls prevalence

30% of ≥65 fall at least once/year; 50% of ≥80; 10-15% result in serious injury

Falls consequences

Fractures (especially hip), head injury, hospitalisation, loss of independence, death

Falls risk factors (intrinsic)

Age, female, previous falls, gait/balance disorder, weakness, cognitive impairment, vision, postural hypotension, incontinence

Falls risk factors (extrinsic)

Polypharmacy (especially sedatives, antihypertensives, hypoglycaemics), environment, footwear

Medication risk

Benzodiazepines, antipsychotics, opioids, antihypertensives, anticholinergics

Falls assessment

History, examination (gait, balance, vision, neuro, CVS), medication review, home assessment

Get Up and Go test

Timed up-and-go; ≥12 seconds = ↑ falls risk

Tinetti balance assessment

Balance and gait scoring; <19 = high falls risk

Falls intervention

Multifactorial: exercise (Otago), home safety, medication review, vitamin D, vision correction

Falls prevention

Tai chi, strength/balance training, home modifications, podiatry

A: Fractures (especially hip), head injury, hospitalisation, loss of independence, death

Q: Falls risk factors (intrinsic)?

A: Age, female, previous falls, gait/balance disorder, weakness, cognitive impairment, vision, postural

Q: Falls risk factors (extrinsic)?

A: Polypharmacy (especially sedatives, antihypertensives, hypoglycaemics), environment, footwear

Q: Medication risk?

A: Benzodiazepines, antipsychotics, opioids, antihypertensives, anticholinergics

Q: Falls assessment?

A: History, examination (gait, balance, vision, neuro, CVS), medication review, home assessment

Q: Get Up and Go test?

A: Timed up-and-go; ≥12 seconds = ↑ falls risk

Q: Tinetti balance assessment?

A: Balance and gait scoring; <19 = high falls risk

Answer Key with Explanations

  4. D — Falls risk factors (extrinsic)

▸ 32.4.2 Falls Prevention And Multifactorial Intervention

Definition

Falls prevention in the elderly is a multifactorial, multidisciplinary approach addressing intrinsic (person-related) and extrinsic (environment-related) risk factors, with evidence supporting exercise, home modifications, medication review, and vision correction as effective interventions.

Core Clinical Content

Multifactorial falls assessment

Identify all modifiable risk factors; address each

Evidence-based interventions

Exercise (Otago, tai chi), home safety, vitamin D, multifactorial intervention

Otago exercise programme

Strength and balance training; ↓ falls by 30-50%

Tai chi

Improves balance, strength, confidence; effective in community-dwelling elderly

Home safety assessment

Remove hazards (loose rugs, poor lighting, clutter), grab rails, non-slip mats

Medication review

Reduce/stop psychotropics, sedatives, antihypertensives, anticholinergics

Vitamin D supplementation

For those with deficiency or limited sun exposure; may ↓ falls

Vision correction

Cataract surgery; treat refractive errors; avoid multifocal glasses when walking

Foot care and footwear

Podiatry; appropriate shoes (low heel, non-slip sole, supportive)

Falls clinics

Multidisciplinary assessment and intervention; evidence-based

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point

Tai chiImproves balance, strength, confidence; effective in community-dwelling elderly

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Falls prevention and multifactorial intervention?

Falls prevention in the elderly is a multifactorial, multidisciplinary approach addressing intrinsic (person-related) and extrinsic (environment-related) risk factors, with evidence supporting exercise, home modifications, medication review, and vision correction as effective interventions.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Falls prevention and multifactorial intervention?

Key features include: multifactorial falls assessment, evidence-based interventions, otago exercise programme.

Q3. How is Falls prevention and multifactorial intervention diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Falls prevention and multifactorial intervention?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Falls prevention and multifactorial intervention?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Falls prevention and multifactorial intervention — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.4.3 Inpatient Falls And Post Fall Assessment

Definition

Inpatient falls are events in which a patient comes to rest inadvertently on the ground or floor during hospitalisation. They are common, often preventable, and associated with increased morbidity, prolonged hospital stay, and increased healthcare costs. Post-fall assessment is a structured approach to identify causes and prevent recurrence.

Core Clinical Content

Inpatient falls prevalence

2-7 falls/1000 bed days; common in elderly; 30% result in injury

Consequences

Injury (1-3% fractures), ↑ LOS, ↑ costs, loss of confidence, litigation

Risk factors in hospital

Unfamiliar environment, ↓ mobility, IV lines/drains, polypharmacy, delirium, urgency

Fall risk assessment

STRATIFY, Morse Falls Scale, Hendrich II; on admission and regularly

Multi-factorial prevention

Bed/chair alarms, non-slip footwear, frequent toileting, medication review, supervision

Baywatching

1:1 supervision for high-risk patients; resource-intensive

Post-fall assessment

ABCDE approach; check for injury (especially hip, head, fractures); CT head if indicated

Investigations after fall

ECG, bloods (FBC, U&E, HbA1c, Ca, ECG), urinalysis, sepsis screen; imaging as indicated

Documentation

Time, place, mechanism, witness, injuries, post-fall condition; risk assessment; action plan

Post-fall review (within 24 h)

MDT review; identify cause; modify risk factors; communicate with patient/family

▸ 32.4.4 Syncope And Presyncope In The Elderly

Definition

Syncope is a transient loss of consciousness due to cerebral hypoperfusion, with rapid onset, short duration, and spontaneous recovery. In the elderly, syncope is often multifactorial and may present atypically (falls, confusion) rather than as a clear blackout.

Core Clinical Content

Syncope definition

TLOC due to cerebral hypoperfusion; rapid onset, short, spontaneous recovery

Syncope prevalence

3-5% of ED visits; ↑ with age; 1-2% of elderly per year

Reflex (vasovagal) syncope

Triggered by emotion, pain, prolonged standing; prodrome (pallor, nausea, sweating)

Orthostatic hypotension

SBP ↓ ≥20 mmHg or DBP ≥10 mmHg within 3 min standing

OH causes

Drugs (antihypertensives, diuretics), autonomic failure, dehydration, prolonged bed rest

Cardiac syncope

Arrhythmia (VT, bradycardia), structural (AS, HOCM, PE, tamponade); ↑ mortality

Cardiac red flags

Exertional, no prodrome, abnormal ECG, known cardiac disease, family SCD

Postprandial hypotension

Common in elderly; ↓ BP after meals due to splanchnic pooling

Investigation

ECG, orthostatic BP, 24h Holter, echo; tilt-table if recurrent unexplained

Orthostatic hypotension management

Hydration, review drugs, ↑ salt, compression stockings, midodrine, fludrocortisone

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
Syncope definitionTLOC due to cerebral hypoperfusion; rapid onset, short, spontaneous recovery
Syncope prevalence3-5% of ED visits; ↑ with age; 1-2% of elderly per year
Reflex (vasovagal) syncopeTriggered by emotion, pain, prolonged standing; prodrome (pallor, nausea, sweati
Orthostatic hypotensionSBP ↓ ≥20 mmHg or DBP ≥10 mmHg within 3 min standing
OH causesDrugs (antihypertensives, diuretics), autonomic failure, dehydration, prolonged
Cardiac syncopeArrhythmia (VT, bradycardia), structural (AS, HOCM, PE, tamponade); ↑ mortality
Cardiac red flagsExertional, no prodrome, abnormal ECG, known cardiac disease, family SCD
Postprandial hypotensionCommon in elderly; ↓ BP after meals due to splanchnic pooling
InvestigationECG, orthostatic BP, 24h Holter, echo; tilt-table if recurrent unexplained
Orthostatic hypotension managementHydration, review drugs, ↑ salt, compression stockings, midodrine, fludrocortiso

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Syncope and presyncope in the elderly?

Syncope is a transient loss of consciousness due to cerebral hypoperfusion, with rapid onset, short duration, and spontaneous recovery. In the elderly, syncope is often multifactorial and may present atypically (falls, confusion) rather than as a clear blackout.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Syncope and presyncope in the elderly?

Key features include: syncope definition, syncope prevalence, reflex (vasovagal) syncope.

Q3. How is Syncope and presyncope in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Syncope and presyncope in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Syncope and presyncope in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Syncope and presyncope in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.5 Frailty and Comprehensive Geriatric Assessment 4
▸ 32.5.1 Comprehensive Geriatric Assessment Cga

Definition

Comprehensive geriatric assessment (CGA) is a multidimensional, multidisciplinary diagnostic process used to determine an older person's medical, psychological, and functional capabilities, with the goal of developing a coordinated and integrated plan for treatment and long-term follow-up.

Core Clinical Content

CGA definition

Multidimensional, multidisciplinary assessment of older adults

CGA domains

Medical, functional (ADL, IADL), cognitive, psychological, social, environmental, spiritual

CGA team

Geriatrician, nurse, OT, PT, social worker, pharmacist, dietitian

ADL assessment

Basic activities: bathing, dressing, toileting, transferring, continence, feeding

IADL assessment

Instrumental: shopping, cooking, housekeeping, finances, medications, transport

Barthel Index

Standardised ADL assessment; 0-20 scale; lower = more dependent

Lawton IADL

Standardised IADL assessment; 0-8 scale

Mini-Mental State Examination (MMSE)

30-point cognitive screen; <24 suggests dementia

Clock drawing test

Quick screen for visuospatial/executive function

CGA benefits

↓ Mortality, ↑ function, ↓ hospitalisation, ↓ NH placement; evidence-based

IADL assessmentInstrumental: shopping, cooking, housekeeping, finances, medications, transport

CGA benefits↓ Mortality, ↑ function, ↓ hospitalisation, ↓ NH placement; evidence-based

▸ 32.5.2 Frailty Syndrome

Definition

Frailty is a distinctive health state related to the ageing process in which multiple body systems gradually lose their inbuilt reserves, characterised by increased vulnerability to stressors, reduced physiological reserve, and increased risk of adverse outcomes including disability, falls, hospitalisation, and death.

Core Clinical Content

Frailty definition

Geriatric syndrome of decreased reserve and resistance to stressors; multi-system

Frailty prevalence

10% of ≥65, 25-50% of ≥85; more common in women, low SES

Frailty models

Phenotype (Fried): 5 criteria; deficit accumulation (Rockwood/Mitnitski)

Fried frailty criteria (≥3 of 5)

Weight loss (unintentional), exhaustion, weakness (grip), slow walking speed, low physical activity

Pre-frailty

1-2 of Fried criteria; reversible with intervention

Rockwood Frailty Index

Deficit accumulation: count of health deficits; continuous score (0-1)

Clinical Frailty Scale (CFS)

9-point scale; 1 (very fit) to 9 (terminally ill); widely used in clinical practice

Pathophysiology

↓ Muscle mass, ↓ bone density, ↓ cognition, ↓ immunity, ↑ inflammation

Outcomes

↑ Falls, ↑ fractures, ↑ hospitalisation, ↑ disability, ↑ mortality

Management

Exercise (resistance + aerobic), protein intake, vitamin D, polypharmacy review; CGA

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Frailty syndrome?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Frailty syndrome?

D) 9-point scale; 1 (very fit) to 9 (terminally ill); widely used in clinical practice

Answer: D

Flashcards

Q: Frailty definition?

A: Geriatric syndrome of decreased reserve and resistance to stressors; multi-system

Q: Frailty prevalence?

A: 10% of ≥65, 25-50% of ≥85; more common in women, low SES

Q: Frailty models?

A: Phenotype (Fried): 5 criteria; deficit accumulation (Rockwood/Mitnitski)

Q: Fried frailty criteria (≥3 of 5)?

A: Weight loss (unintentional), exhaustion, weakness (grip), slow walking speed, low physical activity

Q: Pre-frailty?

A: 1-2 of Fried criteria; reversible with intervention

Q: Rockwood Frailty Index?

A: Deficit accumulation: count of health deficits; continuous score (0-1)

Q: Clinical Frailty Scale (CFS)?

A: 9-point scale; 1 (very fit) to 9 (terminally ill); widely used in clinical practice

Q: Pathophysiology?

A: ↓ Muscle mass, ↓ bone density, ↓ cognition, ↓ immunity, ↑ inflammation

▸ 32.5.3 Multimorbidity And Non Specific Presentations In T

Definition

Multimorbidity is the coexistence of ≥2 chronic conditions in an individual. Older adults often present with non-specific presentations (NSPs) such as functional decline, falls, confusion, or immobility, rather than the classic organ-specific symptoms, making diagnosis challenging.

Core Clinical Content

Multimorbidity definition

≥2 chronic conditions in one person; common in older adults

Multimorbidity prevalence

≥50% of ≥65; ↑ with age; commonest: HTN, OA, CHD, DM, COPD, CKD

Non-specific presentations (NSPs)

Functional decline, falls, immobility, confusion, incontinence, pain

Why NSPs?

Multiple comorbidities, atypical presentation, polypharmacy, age-related physiological changes

Atypical presentation of disease

MI without chest pain, UTI without dysuria, pneumonia without cough

Non-specific presentations in elderly

Often present with 'off legs', falls, confusion, dehydration, weight loss

Differential of NSP

Infection (UTI, pneumonia), dehydration, electrolyte disturbance, medication, MI, stroke, DVT

Comprehensive approach

Don't focus on single organ; full history, examination, medication review

Importance of collateral history

Essential; patient may have cognitive impairment or communication difficulty

Management

Treat reversible causes; CGA; multidisciplinary team; avoid over-medicalisation

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
Multimorbidity definition≥2 chronic conditions in one person; common in older adults
Multimorbidity prevalence≥50% of ≥65; ↑ with age; commonest: HTN, OA, CHD, DM, COPD, CKD
Non-specific presentations (NSPs)Functional decline, falls, immobility, confusion, incontinence, pain
Why NSPs?Multiple comorbidities, atypical presentation, polypharmacy, age-related physiol

Differential of NSPInfection (UTI, pneumonia), dehydration, electrolyte disturbance, medication, MI
Comprehensive approachDon't focus on single organ; full history, examination, medication review
Importance of collateral historyEssential; patient may have cognitive impairment or communication difficulty
ManagementTreat reversible causes; CGA; multidisciplinary team; avoid over-medicalisation

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Multimorbidity and non-specific presentations in the elderly?

Multimorbidity is the coexistence of ≥2 chronic conditions in an individual. Older adults often present with non-specific presentations (NSPs) such as functional decline, falls, confusion, or immobility, rather than the classic organ-specific symptoms, making diagnosis challenging.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Multimorbidity and non-specific presentations in the elderly?

Key features include: multimorbidity definition, multimorbidity prevalence, non-specific presentations (nsps).

Q3. How is Multimorbidity and non-specific presentations in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Multimorbidity and non-specific presentations in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Multimorbidity and non-specific presentations in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Multimorbidity and non-specific presentations in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.5.4 Sarcopenia And Muscle Ageing

Definition

Sarcopenia is a progressive, generalised skeletal muscle disorder involving accelerated loss of muscle mass and function, associated with increased adverse outcomes including falls, frailty, functional decline, and mortality. It is a major component of frailty and is increasingly common with age.

Core Clinical Content

Sarcopenia definition

Progressive loss of skeletal muscle mass and function; ageing-related

Sarcopenia prevalence

5-13% of 60-70 years; 11-50% of ≥80 years

Primary sarcopenia

Age-related; no other identifiable cause

Secondary sarcopenia

Due to disease (cancer, CHF, COPD), inactivity, malnutrition, endocrine

Diagnosis (EWGSOP2)

Low muscle strength (grip/chair stand) + low muscle quantity/quality; severity by performance

Sarcopenic obesity

Low muscle mass + high fat mass; particularly in older adults; worse outcomes

Pathophysiology

↓ anabolic hormones (testosterone, GH, IGF-1), ↑ inflammation, ↓ protein synthesis, ↑ apoptosis

Anorexia of ageing

↓ appetite, ↓ taste/smell, ↓ gastric motility; contributes to sarcopenia

Management

Resistance exercise (most effective), protein intake 1.2-1.5 g/kg/day, vitamin D

Outcomes

↑ Falls, ↑ fractures, ↑ disability, ↑ mortality; reversible with early intervention

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Sarcopenia prevalence5-13% of 60-70 years; 11-50% of ≥80 years

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Sarcopenia and muscle ageing?

Q3. How is Sarcopenia and muscle ageing diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Sarcopenia and muscle ageing?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Sarcopenia and muscle ageing?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

A: Age-related; no other identifiable cause

Q: Secondary sarcopenia?

A: Due to disease (cancer, CHF, COPD), inactivity, malnutrition, endocrine

Q: Diagnosis (EWGSOP2)?

A: Low muscle strength (grip/chair stand) + low muscle quantity/quality; severity by performance

Q: Sarcopenic obesity?

A: Low muscle mass + high fat mass; particularly in older adults; worse outcomes

Q: Pathophysiology?

A: ↓ anabolic hormones (testosterone, GH, IGF-1), ↑ inflammation, ↓ protein synthesis, ↑ apoptosis

Q: Anorexia of ageing?

A: ↓ appetite, ↓ taste/smell, ↓ gastric motility; contributes to sarcopenia

Answer Key with Explanations

  2. B — Sarcopenia prevalence

  4. D — Secondary sarcopenia
  5. A — Diagnosis (EWGSOP2)
  6. B — Sarcopenic obesity
  7. C — Pathophysiology
  8. D — Anorexia of ageing
  9. A — Management

▸ 32.6 Legal, Ethical, and Advance Care Planning 3
▸ 32.6.1 Advance Care Planning And End Of Life Decision Mak

Definition

Advance care planning is the process of discussing and documenting an individual's preferences for future medical care, particularly at the end of life, while they retain capacity. It includes advance decisions to refuse treatment, lasting power of attorney, and other directives.

Core Clinical Content

Advance care planning (ACP)

Process of discussing and documenting future care preferences while patient has capacity

ACP benefits

Respects autonomy; ↓ unwanted treatment; ↓ family distress; ↓ conflict

When to discuss

Early in disease course; routinely for chronic/progressive; annually review

Advance Decision to Refuse Treatment (ADRT)

Legally binding; specific treatment refused; applies when patient loses capacity

ADRT validity

≥18, capacity, voluntary, informed, specific; ADRT for life-sustaining treatment must be in writing with witness

Lasting Power of Attorney (LPA)

Property/financial LPA + Health/welfare LPA; registered; acts when patient loses capacity

Best Interests Decision

For those without capacity and no LPA/ADRT; multidisciplinary; considers past/present wishes

Do Not Attempt Cardiopulmonary Resuscitation (DNACPR)

Documented decision; respects autonomy; for patients with capacity to refuse

Communication

Discuss with patient, family, MDT; document clearly; share with all relevant parties

ReSPECT process

Recommended Summary Plan for Emergency Care and Treatment; UK; replaces DNACPR form

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Advance care planning and end-of-life decision-making?

Advance care planning is the process of discussing and documenting an individual's preferences for future medical care, particularly at the end of life, while they retain capacity. It includes advance decisions to refuse treatment, lasting power of attorney, and other directives.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Advance care planning and end-of-life decision-making?

Key features include: advance care planning (acp), acp benefits, when to discuss.

Q3. How is Advance care planning and end-of-life decision-making diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Advance care planning and end-of-life decision-making?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Advance care planning and end-of-life decision-making?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Advance care planning and end-of-life decision-making — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.6.2 Driving Assessment In The Elderly

Definition

Driving assessment in older adults involves evaluating fitness to drive in the context of age-related physiological changes, medical conditions, and medications. It is an important clinical and legal issue with implications for independence, safety, and quality of life.

Core Clinical Content

Driving in elderly

Independent mobility important; but age-related changes ↑ accident risk

DVLA notification (UK)

Patient must notify DVLA of any medical condition that may affect driving; doctor should advise patient

Doctor's duty

Advise patient to notify DVLA; if patient refuses AND public safety at risk, break confidentiality

Dementia and driving

DVLA must be notified; usually cease driving; MMSE alone insufficient; on-road assessment

Epilepsy and driving

1 year seizure-free for ordinary licence; 6 months for vocational

Diabetes and driving

Insulin-treated diabetics: notify DVLA; must not drive if hypoglycaemia unawareness

Cardiac conditions and driving

MI: 4 weeks off; angina if at rest: cease; ICD: 6 months off

Stroke/TIA and driving

1 month off for stroke; 2 weeks for single TIA; neurological assessment

Visual acuity requirement

UK: 6/9 (Snellen) + 6/12 both eyes; visual field ≥120°

Medication and driving

Sedatives, opioids, anticonvulsants may impair; counsel patients

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Driving assessment in the elderly?

Driving assessment in older adults involves evaluating fitness to drive in the context of age-related physiological changes, medical conditions, and medications. It is an important clinical and legal issue with implications for independence, safety, and quality of life.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Driving assessment in the elderly?

Key features include: driving in elderly, dvla notification (uk), doctor's duty.

Q3. How is Driving assessment in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Driving assessment in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Driving assessment in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Driving assessment in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.6.3 Elder Abuse And Safeguarding

Definition

Elder abuse is a single or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust, that causes harm or distress to an older person. It is under-recognised, often hidden, and requires a high index of suspicion and safeguarding procedures.

Core Clinical Content

Elder abuse prevalence

10% of elderly; higher in dementia, disability, dependence; under-recognised

Types of abuse

Physical, psychological, sexual, financial, neglect, self-neglect, discrimination

Risk factors

Carer stress, substance use, mental health, social isolation, cognitive impairment, dependence

Warning signs

Unexplained injuries, malnutrition, dehydration, fear, depression, withdrawal, financial anomalies

Screening questions

Have you been hurt? Are you afraid? Has anyone taken your things without asking?

UK safeguarding

Care Act 2014; section 42 enquiry for adults at risk of abuse/neglect

Duty to report

Health/social care professionals; raising concern; follows local safeguarding procedures

Multi-agency safeguarding

Police, social services, health, voluntary sector; coordinated response

Self-neglect

Often in mental illness, dementia, substance use; requires assessment of capacity, support

Capacity and abuse

If patient has capacity and is not at serious risk, may refuse intervention; document carefully

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
Elder abuse prevalence10% of elderly; higher in dementia, disability, dependence; under-recognised
Types of abusePhysical, psychological, sexual, financial, neglect, self-neglect, discriminatio
Risk factorsCarer stress, substance use, mental health, social isolation, cognitive impairme
Warning signsUnexplained injuries, malnutrition, dehydration, fear, depression, withdrawal, f
Screening questionsHave you been hurt? Are you afraid? Has anyone taken your things without asking?
UK safeguardingCare Act 2014; section 42 enquiry for adults at risk of abuse/neglect
Duty to reportHealth/social care professionals; raising concern; follows local safeguarding pr
Multi-agency safeguardingPolice, social services, health, voluntary sector; coordinated response
Self-neglectOften in mental illness, dementia, substance use; requires assessment of capacit
Capacity and abuseIf patient has capacity and is not at serious risk, may refuse intervention; doc

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Elder abuse and safeguarding?

Elder abuse is a single or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust, that causes harm or distress to an older person. It is under-recognised, often hidden, and requires a high index of suspicion and safeguarding procedures.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Elder abuse and safeguarding?

Key features include: elder abuse prevalence, types of abuse, risk factors.

Q3. How is Elder abuse and safeguarding diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Elder abuse and safeguarding?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Elder abuse and safeguarding?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Elder abuse and safeguarding — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.7 Nutrition and Hydration 3
▸ 32.7.1 Dehydration And Fluid Balance In The Elderly

Definition

Dehydration is a state of negative fluid balance, common in older adults due to reduced thirst sensation, decreased renal concentrating ability, and multiple comorbidities and medications. It is associated with increased morbidity (falls, delirium, AKI) and mortality.

Core Clinical Content

Dehydration prevalence

Common cause of hospitalisation; ↑ morbidity and mortality in elderly

Reduced thirst sensation

↑ Osmoreceptor threshold; ↓ response to hypertonicity; ↑ ADH but ↓ response

Reduced renal function

↓ GFR, ↓ concentrating ability, ↓ Na+ reabsorption; ↑ risk of dehydration and hyponatraemia

Causes of dehydration

↓ intake (swallowing, cognitive, depression), ↑ losses (diuretics, vomiting, diarrhoea, fever)

Hypernatraemic dehydration

Water loss > Na+ loss; ↑ Na+; common in frail, immobile, febrile elderly

Hyponatraemia in elderly

Common; SIADH (SSRIs, antipsychotics), thiazides, heart failure, cirrhosis

Clinical signs of dehydration

Dry mucosa, ↓ skin turgor (unreliable in elderly), tachycardia, hypotension, confusion, oliguria

Diagnosis

Clinical; urea:creatinine ratio >20, ↑ serum Na+ (hypernatraemic), ↑ haematocrit

Management

Oral rehydration if possible; IV 0.9% saline; correct slowly (avoid cerebral oedema)

Prevention

Regular fluid rounds, accessible drinks, address dysphagia, humidified O2

Q3. How is Dehydration and fluid balance in the elderly diagnosed?

▸ 32.7.2 Dysphagia And Enteral Feeding In The Elderly

Definition

Dysphagia is difficulty swallowing, common in older adults due to neurological conditions (stroke, dementia, Parkinson's), structural causes, and medications. It is associated with malnutrition, dehydration, aspiration pneumonia, and increased mortality, requiring comprehensive assessment and management.

Core Clinical Content

Dysphagia prevalence

30-40% of stroke, 50-80% of advanced dementia, 30% of Parkinson's

Dysphagia causes (elderly)

Stroke, dementia, Parkinson's, motor neurone disease, head and neck cancer, strictures, achalasia

Oropharyngeal vs oesophageal

Oropharyngeal: initiation problem (stroke, dementia); oesophageal: transit problem (stricture, motility)

Bedside swallowing assessment

Sips of water (3 oz test); observe for cough, wet voice, delayed swallow

Videofluoroscopy (VFS)

Gold standard; modified barium swallow; assess oral, pharyngeal, oesophageal phases

FEES

Fibreoptic endoscopic evaluation of swallowing; bedside; real-time visualisation

Aspiration pneumonia

Common complication; risks: ↓ consciousness, dysphagia, ↓ cough, NG tube, poor oral hygiene

Dysphagia diet modification

Texture modification (soft, pureed); thickened fluids; chin-tuck, head turn

NG tube feeding

Short-term (<4 weeks); for acute stroke, reversible dysphagia

PEG tube

Long-term (>4 weeks); controversial in advanced dementia; ethical considerations

C) Sips of water (3 oz test); observe for cough, wet voice, delayed swallow
