▸ 32.7.3 Malnutrition And Anorexia Of Ageing

Definition

Malnutrition in the elderly is a state of energy, protein, or micronutrient deficiency that adversely affects body composition, function, and clinical outcomes. Anorexia of ageing is the physiological decline in appetite and food intake with age, contributing to malnutrition, sarcopenia, and frailty in older adults.

Core Clinical Content

Malnutrition prevalence

10-30% of community elderly; 30-50% of hospitalised; 40-70% of nursing home

MUST score

Malnutrition Universal Screening Tool: BMI, weight loss, disease effect; 0-6 score

MNA

Mini Nutritional Assessment; for ≥65; screens malnutrition and risk

Anorexia of ageing

↓ Appetite with age; ↓ taste/smell, ↓ gastric motility, ↑ satiety hormones (CCK, leptin)

Causes of malnutrition in elderly

Anorexia of ageing, depression, dementia, dysphagia, dental, drugs, social isolation

Consequences

↓ Immune function, ↓ wound healing, ↑ falls, ↑ fractures, ↑ mortality

Refeeding syndrome

Hypophosphataemia, hypokalaemia, hypomagnesaemia, fluid overload with reintroduction of nutrition

Refeeding prevention

Start slowly (10-20 kcal/kg/day), supplement thiamine, monitor electrolytes

Management

Nutritional support (oral, NG, PEG, IV); food fortification; assist with feeding; consider appetite stimulants

Vitamin D

Common deficiency; 800-2000 IU daily; especially in nursing home and housebound

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
Malnutrition prevalence10-30% of community elderly; 30-50% of hospitalised; 40-70% of nursing home
MUST scoreMalnutrition Universal Screening Tool: BMI, weight loss, disease effect; 0-6 sco

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Malnutrition and anorexia of ageing?

Malnutrition in the elderly is a state of energy, protein, or micronutrient deficiency that adversely affects body composition, function, and clinical outcomes. Anorexia of ageing is the physiological decline in appetite and food intake with age, contributing to malnutrition, sarcopenia, and frailty in older adults.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Malnutrition and anorexia of ageing?

Key features include: malnutrition prevalence, must score, mna.

Q3. How is Malnutrition and anorexia of ageing diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Malnutrition and anorexia of ageing?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Malnutrition and anorexia of ageing?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Malnutrition and anorexia of ageing — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.8 Palliative and End-of-Life Care 3
▸ 32.8.1 End Of Life Care And The Dying Patient

Definition

End-of-life care is the support and medical care given during the time surrounding death, focusing on comfort, dignity, and quality of life, addressing physical, psychological, social, and spiritual needs of the patient and their family.

Core Clinical Content

Recognising dying

↓ Consciousness, ↓ oral intake, ↓ mobility, Cheyne-Stokes respiration, mottling

Comfort care

Stop unnecessary medications; essential medications only; subcutaneous route

Symptom management in dying

Pain, dyspnoea, agitation, respiratory secretions, nausea — regular review

Respiratory secretions ('death rattle')

Noisy breathing; repositioning, hyoscine butylbromide, glycopyrronium

Terminal agitation

Midazolam SC; haloperidol if delirium; review underlying cause

End-of-life care pathway

Individualised; document decisions; regular review; family communication

Liverpool Care Pathway (LCP)

Replaced by individualised end-of-life care plans; principles: comfort, dignity, communication

Family support

Information, support, practical help, bereavement support

Spiritual care

Address spiritual needs; chaplains, faith leaders; cultural sensitivity

Death verification

Absence of cardiorespiratory function for 5 min; pupil fixed; no response to stimuli

C) ↓ Consciousness, ↓ oral intake, ↓ mobility, Cheyne-Stokes respiration, mottling

D) Stop unnecessary medications; essential medications only; subcutaneous route

Answer: A

  1. Key feature regarding End-of-life care and the dying patient?

A) Absence of cardiorespiratory function for 5 min; pupil fixed; no response to stimuli

B) ↓ Consciousness, ↓ oral intake, ↓ mobility, Cheyne-Stokes respiration, mottling

C) Stop unnecessary medications; essential medications only; subcutaneous route

D) Pain, dyspnoea, agitation, respiratory secretions, nausea — regular review

Answer: B

Flashcards

Q: Recognising dying?

A: ↓ Consciousness, ↓ oral intake, ↓ mobility, Cheyne-Stokes respiration, mottling

Q: Comfort care?

A: Stop unnecessary medications; essential medications only; subcutaneous route

Q: Symptom management in dying?

A: Pain, dyspnoea, agitation, respiratory secretions, nausea — regular review

Q: Respiratory secretions ('death rattle')?

A: Noisy breathing; repositioning, hyoscine butylbromide, glycopyrronium

Q: Terminal agitation?

A: Midazolam SC; haloperidol if delirium; review underlying cause

Q: End-of-life care pathway?

A: Individualised; document decisions; regular review; family communication

Q: Liverpool Care Pathway (LCP)?

A: Replaced by individualised end-of-life care plans; principles: comfort, dignity, communication

Q: Family support?

A: Information, support, practical help, bereavement support

▸ 32.8.2 Pain Management In The Elderly

Definition

Pain management in the elderly requires careful assessment, consideration of comorbidities, polypharmacy, and altered pharmacokinetics, with appropriate use of the WHO analgesic ladder and non-pharmacological approaches. Pain is often under-recognised and under-treated in older adults.

Core Clinical Content

Pain prevalence

25-50% of community elderly; 50-80% of nursing home; chronic pain common

Pain types

Nociceptive (somatic, visceral), neuropathic, mixed; multiple causes common

Pain assessment

Verbal rating, numeric rating, faces; in dementia: PAINAD, Abbey Pain Scale, Doloplus-2

WHO analgesic ladder

Step 1: paracetamol ± adjuvant; Step 2: weak opioid (codeine, tramadol); Step 3: morphine

Paracetamol

First-line; safe in elderly; max 4 g/day (3 g in frail, liver disease, malnutrition)

NSAIDs

Use cautiously; ↑ GI bleeding, AKI, cardiovascular risk; shortest course possible

Opioids in elderly

Start low, go slow; oral preferred; morphine first-line; laxative, antiemetic

Neuropathic pain

Amitriptyline (cautious in elderly), gabapentin, pregabalin, duloxetine

Non-pharmacological

Physiotherapy, TENS, acupuncture, CBT, social support

Opioid side effects

Constipation, nausea, sedation, falls, respiratory depression; respiratory depression rare if titrated

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Pain management in the elderly?

Pain management in the elderly requires careful assessment, consideration of comorbidities, polypharmacy, and altered pharmacokinetics, with appropriate use of the WHO analgesic ladder and non-pharmacological approaches. Pain is often under-recognised and under-treated in older adults.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Pain management in the elderly?

Key features include: pain prevalence, pain types, pain assessment.

Q3. How is Pain management in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Pain management in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Pain management in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Pain management in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • 'SSKIN' for pressure ulcer prevention: Surface, Skin inspection, Keep moving, Incontinence, Nutrition
  • 'WHO analgesic ladder' — Step 1: paracetamol; Step 2: weak opioid; Step 3: strong opioid
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly
  • 'BPSD' — Behavioural and Psychological Symptoms of Dementia

▸ 32.8.3 Palliative Care In The Elderly

Definition

Palliative care in the elderly is an approach that improves quality of life of patients and families facing life-limiting illness, through prevention and relief of suffering by early identification, assessment, and treatment of pain and other physical, psychosocial, and spiritual problems.

Core Clinical Content

Palliative care definition

Approach improving quality of life; physical, psychosocial, spiritual care

Palliative care in elderly

Not just cancer; CHF, COPD, dementia, frailty, multi-organ failure all benefit

When to refer

Any life-limiting illness; complex symptoms; difficult decisions; end-of-life care

Symptom management

Pain, dyspnoea, nausea, constipation, delirium, anxiety, depression, fatigue

WHO analgesic ladder

Step 1: non-opioid (paracetamol) ± adjuvant; Step 2: weak opioid (codeine, tramadol); Step 3: strong opioid (morphine)

Opioid prescribing in elderly

Start low, go slow; oral preferred; laxative; antiemetic; regular review

Breathlessness

Non-pharmacological (positioning, fan); opioids (morphine); benzodiazepines if anxiety

Constipation

Common; prophylactic laxatives with opioids

Delirium in palliative care

Common; causes: drugs, metabolic, infection; manage underlying cause if possible

Palliative care emergencies

Spinal cord compression, hypercalcaemia, haemorrhage, SVC syndrome — treat with steroids, etc.

▸ 32.9 Polypharmacy and Pharmacology 5
▸ 32.9.1 Adverse Drug Reactions In The Elderly

Definition

Adverse drug reactions (ADRs) are harmful, unintended responses to medications, occurring at doses normally used in man. In the elderly, ADRs are common due to polypharmacy, altered pharmacokinetics, and increased vulnerability, and may present atypically (confusion, falls) rather than with the classic drug-specific symptoms.

Core Clinical Content

ADR prevalence

10-20% of hospital admissions in elderly; ↑ with polypharmacy

Atypical ADR presentation

Confusion, falls, immobility, incontinence, constipation, weight loss

Common ADRs in elderly

Hypotension (antihypertensives), falls (sedatives), confusion (anticholinergics), bleeding (warfarin)

Anticholinergic ADR

Confusion, dry mouth, blurred vision, urinary retention, constipation

Bleeding risk

Warfarin, DOACs, antiplatelets, NSAIDs; ↑ in elderly

Hypoglycaemia

Insulin, sulfonylureas (long-acting); unpredictable; severe consequences

SIADH

SSRIs, antipsychotics, anticonvulsants; ↓ Na+ in elderly

Falls-inducing drugs

Benzodiazepines, antipsychotics, opioids, antihypertensives, diuretics, anticholinergics

Acute kidney injury

NSAIDs, ACEi, diuretics ('triple whammy'); common in dehydration

Prevention of ADRs

Medication review, start low go slow, patient/carer education, monitoring

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point

Prevention of ADRsMedication review, start low go slow, patient/carer education, monitoring

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Adverse drug reactions in the elderly?

Adverse drug reactions (ADRs) are harmful, unintended responses to medications, occurring at doses normally used in man. In the elderly, ADRs are common due to polypharmacy, altered pharmacokinetics, and increased vulnerability, and may present atypically (confusion, falls) rather than with the classic drug-specific symptoms.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Adverse drug reactions in the elderly?

Key features include: adr prevalence, atypical adr presentation, common adrs in elderly.

Q3. How is Adverse drug reactions in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Adverse drug reactions in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Adverse drug reactions in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Adverse drug reactions in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.9.2 Anticholinergic Burden And Toxicity In The Elderly

Definition

Anticholinergic burden in the elderly refers to the cumulative effect of multiple medications with anticholinergic properties, leading to cognitive impairment, falls, urinary retention, constipation, and other adverse effects. It is a major and modifiable cause of harm in older adults and is often under-recognised.

Core Clinical Content

Anticholinergic burden definition

Cumulative anticholinergic effect of all medications; common in polypharmacy

Prevalence

20-50% of elderly take ≥1 anticholinergic medication; often multiple

Common anticholinergic drugs

Antihistamines (chlorpheniramine), TCAs (amitriptyline), bladder antispasmodics (oxybutynin), antipsychotics

Anticholinergic effects

Central: confusion, ↓ cognition, hallucinations; Peripheral: dry mouth, blurred vision, constipation, urinary retention, tachycardia

CNS effects

Confusion, delirium, cognitive decline, ↓ memory; contributes to dementia risk

Falls risk

↓ Cognitive function, ↓ postural stability, blurred vision; ↑ falls

Anticholinergic Cognitive Burden (ACB) scale

Tool to quantify anticholinergic burden; each drug scored 0-3

Common ACB 3 drugs (high burden)

Amitriptyline, oxybutynin, chlorpheniramine, hydroxyzine, olanzapine, paroxetine

Deprescribing anticholinergics

Switch to alternatives (SSRI, mirabegron); consider dose reduction; non-pharmacological where possible

Anticholinergic syndrome (acute)

Hot as a hare, dry as a bone, red as a beet, blind as a bat, mad as a hatter, full as a flask

It is a major and modifiable cause of harm in older adults and is often under-recognised.

  • 'SIG E CAPS' for depression in elderly (atypical presentation common)

  • 'Hot as a hare, dry as a bone, red as a beet, blind as a bat, mad as a hatter' — anticholinergic syndrome
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.9.3 Deprescribing And Systematic Medication Review

Definition

Deprescribing is the systematic process of identifying and discontinuing medications where harms outweigh benefits, with the goal of reducing polypharmacy and adverse drug reactions while maintaining or improving quality of life. It is a key component of geriatric care.

Core Clinical Content

Deprescribing definition

Supervised withdrawal of inappropriate medications; shared decision-making

When to consider

Polypharmacy, ADRs, falls, cognitive decline, end-of-life, patient request

Benefits of deprescribing

↓ ADRs, ↓ falls, ↓ pill burden, ↑ adherence, cost savings, improved QoL

Barriers

Patient/carer resistance, prescriber inertia, fear of withdrawal, lack of evidence

Step 1: Identify

List all medications; identify indication, effectiveness, safety, adherence

Step 2: Decide

Which medications can be stopped? Consider patient goals, life expectancy

Step 3: Plan

Withdraw one drug at a time; start with lowest risk (e.g., supplements, PPIs)

Step 4: Implement

Taper slowly (especially long-half-life drugs); monitor for withdrawal/rebound

Step 5: Review

Monitor for benefit (↓ symptoms) or harm (withdrawal, disease recurrence)

Common drugs to deprescribe

PPIs (>8 weeks), benzodiazepines, anticholinergics, statins in limited life expectancy

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

TopicKey Point
Deprescribing definitionSupervised withdrawal of inappropriate medications; shared decision-making
When to considerPolypharmacy, ADRs, falls, cognitive decline, end-of-life, patient request
Benefits of deprescribing↓ ADRs, ↓ falls, ↓ pill burden, ↑ adherence, cost savings, improved QoL
BarriersPatient/carer resistance, prescriber inertia, fear of withdrawal, lack of eviden
Step 1: IdentifyList all medications; identify indication, effectiveness, safety, adherence
Step 2: DecideWhich medications can be stopped? Consider patient goals, life expectancy
Step 3: PlanWithdraw one drug at a time; start with lowest risk (e.g., supplements, PPIs)
Step 4: ImplementTaper slowly (especially long-half-life drugs); monitor for withdrawal/rebound
Step 5: ReviewMonitor for benefit (↓ symptoms) or harm (withdrawal, disease recurrence)
Common drugs to deprescribePPIs (>8 weeks), benzodiazepines, anticholinergics, statins in limited life expe

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Deprescribing and systematic medication review?

Deprescribing is the systematic process of identifying and discontinuing medications where harms outweigh benefits, with the goal of reducing polypharmacy and adverse drug reactions while maintaining or improving quality of life. It is a key component of geriatric care.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Deprescribing and systematic medication review?

Key features include: deprescribing definition, when to consider, benefits of deprescribing.

Q3. How is Deprescribing and systematic medication review diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Deprescribing and systematic medication review?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Deprescribing and systematic medication review?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Deprescribing and systematic medication review — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.9.4 Pharmacology And Prescribing In The Elderly

Definition

Ageing causes pharmacokinetic and pharmacodynamic changes that alter drug handling and response. Prescribing in the elderly requires careful consideration of these changes, renal/hepatic function, comorbidities, polypharmacy, and patient preferences to optimise efficacy and minimise harm.

Core Clinical Content

Pharmacokinetics changes

↓ absorption (less effect), ↓ body water (↑ concentration water-soluble), ↑ fat (↑ retention lipophilic), ↓ lean mass

Renal function decline

↓ GFR (~1 mL/min/year after 30); ↓ tubular function; ↑ half-life renally-cleared drugs

Creatinine clearance estimation

Cockcroft-Gault (most accurate in elderly); eGFR overestimates in frail elderly

Hepatic metabolism

↓ Phase I (oxidation, CYP450) more than Phase II (conjugation)

Pharmacodynamic changes

↑ sensitivity to drugs (e.g., warfarin, opioids, benzodiazepines); ↓ homeostatic reserve

Anticholinergic burden

Sum of anticholinergic effects of all medications; ↑ falls, confusion, constipation

Beers criteria PIMs

Long-acting benzodiazepines, anticholinergics, antipsychotics in dementia

START criteria

Under-prescribing: ACEi in CHF, bisphosphonates in osteoporosis, vitamin D in deficiency

Number needed to treat (NNT)

↑ with age; may exceed life expectancy in very elderly

Start low, go slow

Principle for prescribing in elderly; titrate gradually; review regularly

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Pharmacology and prescribing in the elderly?

Ageing causes pharmacokinetic and pharmacodynamic changes that alter drug handling and response. Prescribing in the elderly requires careful consideration of these changes, renal/hepatic function, comorbidities, polypharmacy, and patient preferences to optimise efficacy and minimise harm.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Pharmacology and prescribing in the elderly?

Key features include: pharmacokinetics changes, renal function decline, creatinine clearance estimation.

Q3. How is Pharmacology and prescribing in the elderly diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Pharmacology and prescribing in the elderly?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Pharmacology and prescribing in the elderly?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

  • Mnemonic for Pharmacology and prescribing in the elderly — see Core Clinical Content key points

  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

▸ 32.9.5 Polypharmacy And Inappropriate Prescribing

Definition

Polypharmacy is the concurrent use of multiple medications by a patient, commonly defined as ≥5 medications. Inappropriate prescribing is when the risks outweigh the benefits or when a better alternative exists; common in older adults and associated with adverse outcomes including falls, hospitalisation, and mortality.

Core Clinical Content

Polypharmacy definition

≥5 medications; hyperpolypharmacy ≥10

Polypharmacy prevalence

40-50% of ≥65; ↑ with age, multimorbidity, polypharmacy clinics

Adverse drug reactions (ADRs)

↑ 2x with polypharmacy; 4-5x with hyperpolypharmacy; cause 5-10% hospital admissions

Falls risk ↑ with polypharmacy

Each medication adds to falls risk; cumulative effect significant

Beers Criteria (US)

List of PIMs (potentially inappropriate medications) for older adults; updated regularly

STOPP/START criteria (UK/EU)

Screening Tool of Older Persons' Prescriptions / Screening Tool to Alert to Right Treatment

Inappropriate prescribing examples

Long-term benzodiazepines, anticholinergics in dementia, NSAIDs in CKD

Prescribing cascade

Adverse drug reaction misinterpreted as new condition; new drug added

Deprescribing

Systematic process of identifying and discontinuing inappropriate medications

Tools for deprescribing

Beers, STOPP/START, NO TEARS, medication review with patient/carer

FCPS/MRCP High-Yield Summary

Viva Questions

Q1. What is the definition of Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing?

Polypharmacy is the concurrent use of multiple medications by a patient, commonly defined as ≥5 medications. Inappropriate prescribing is when the risks outweigh the benefits or when a better alternative exists; common in older adults and associated with adverse outcomes including falls, hospitalisation, and mortality.

Q2. What are the key clinical features of Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing?

Key features include: polypharmacy definition, polypharmacy prevalence, adverse drug reactions (adrs).

Q3. How is Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing diagnosed?

Based on comprehensive assessment including history, examination, investigations, and CGA.

Q4. What is the first-line treatment for Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing?

Multi-component intervention: non-pharmacological + pharmacological tailored to individual.

Q5. What is the prognosis of Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing?

Variable; depends on underlying cause, frailty, comorbidities, and access to intervention.

Confusions & Mnemonics

  • Mnemonic for Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing — see Core Clinical Content key points
  • '5 Fried criteria' for frailty: Weight loss, Exhaustion, Weakness, Slow walking, ↓ activity
  • 'DIAPPERS' for transient incontinence: Delirium, Infection, Atrophic vaginitis, Pharmaceuticals, Psychological, Excess urine, Restricted mobility, Stool impaction
  • 'Beers Criteria / STOPP-START' for inappropriate prescribing in elderly
  • 'Start low, go slow' for prescribing in elderly

  1. Key feature regarding Polypharmacy and inappropriate prescribing?

B) 40-50% of ≥65; ↑ with age, multimorbidity, polypharmacy clinics

C) ↑ 2x with polypharmacy; 4-5x with hyperpolypharmacy; cause 5-10% hospital admissions

D) Each medication adds to falls risk; cumulative effect significant

Answer: A

Flashcards

Q: Polypharmacy definition?

A: ≥5 medications; hyperpolypharmacy ≥10

Q: Polypharmacy prevalence?

A: 40-50% of ≥65; ↑ with age, multimorbidity, polypharmacy clinics

Q: Adverse drug reactions (ADRs)?

A: ↑ 2x with polypharmacy; 4-5x with hyperpolypharmacy; cause 5-10% hospital admissions

Q: Falls risk ↑ with polypharmacy?

A: Each medication adds to falls risk; cumulative effect significant

Q: Beers Criteria (US)?

A: List of PIMs (potentially inappropriate medications) for older adults; updated regularly

Q: STOPP/START criteria (UK/EU)?

A: Screening Tool of Older Persons' Prescriptions / Screening Tool to Alert to Right Treatment

Q: Inappropriate prescribing examples?

A: Long-term benzodiazepines, anticholinergics in dementia, NSAIDs in CKD

Q: Prescribing cascade?

A: Adverse drug reaction misinterpreted as new condition; new drug added
